        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>By wOBA Martinez had the best year at the plate of his career (.375).  His always great plate discipline was even better this year, as he walked more than he struck out (75 to 74) for the first time in his career.  In addition, he is the rare low-strikeout hitter who also hits for good power (23 homers and ISO of .177).  The excellent on-base skills, good power, and hitting in the middle of the order in both Cleveland and Boston translated into healthy run and RBI totals (88 and 108).  As he has gotten older, he has spent more time at first base; this past year, he started 82 games at catcher and 66 at first.  His bat is good enough to play at first base, and the time there probably helps to keep him healthy enough to play the whole year.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Martinez is the No. 2 fantasy catcher going into 2010 behind only Joe Mauer.  A full season hitting in the middle of Boston’s high-OBP lineup will help his RBI and runs.  And playing half his games in Fenway will give a boost to his already good average.  With a full season, he should probably hit about .300 with 20 homers, 80 runs, and 100 RBI.  Boston will do everything it can to keep him in the lineup as much as possible, by playing him at first and DH often, as well as at catcher.  His value is even better in walk/OBP (he always walks a ton) and double (Fenway increases two-baggers) leagues.  Entering his age-31 season, Martinez is a stud at the shallowest fantasy position.  (Dave Allen)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>When healthy, Victor Martinez provides plenty of value. That he'll serve as Detroit's primary DH in 2011 means he'll have a better chance of staying on the field. It also means that he has the chance to get far more at-bats than your typical catcher, making him even more valuable at the position. In the past he has been good for 20 HR, 80 R, 80 RBI, and a .300 AVG in under 600 AB. In 2011 he could top that mark, and could therefore see an increase in his HR, R, and RBI. While Joe Mauer will be the first pick among catchers in most leagues, Martinez, because of the increased playing time and reduced physical toll, just might be the best of the bunch in 2011.  (Joe Pawlikowski)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>A move to DH, while retaining catcher eligibility, will greatly enhance Victor Martinez's value. Because of the chance at increased at-bats, he could be the best catcher available. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>As odd as it sounds to say it, given his knee injury and losing of catcher status, Martinez' days as a higher-end fantasy option may be ending. Much of his production was batting-average aided in 2011 (.330 average), and his next game will likely be as a 34-year-old (with 7000-plus innings behind the plate, no less). Even if he returns close to his previous levels, a .300ish average and a dozen or fewer home runs isn't very sexy for a utility/DH slot, and it would seem that a domino effect like that would also bring his RBI opportunities down as well. (Brandon Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>With the recent news of his knee injury, he should be completely off your radar for 2012, and possibly for good as a full-time DH going forward. He's got 7000-plus innings behind the plate in those legs, so he may not come back 100 percent. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The middle-rounds would be a good time for redrafters to target Martinez in 2013 — there the risk is small enough that he could provide surplus value even if he doesn't fully rebound towards his career .303/.370/.469. Prior to the injury, Martinez saw a steady decline in home run production in each of his previous four full seasons (25, 23, 20,12), so potential suitors may not want to select him if you’re team is lacking in the long ball department. But feel confident in the former catcher’s ability to provide steady production in the batting average, runs scored and runs batted in categories for some nice balance on your roto squad. Just make sure he's a catcher before you invest. If he isn't, he's a last-round dollar-type utility play at best. (Alan Harrison)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Martinez missed the entire 2012 season due to a knee injury suffered in offseason workouts, but he’s expected to be fully healed and ready to contribute by the start of spring training. If he’s as healthy as expected, Martinez should be a sure bet for another .300 or better season with double-digit long balls out of the sixth spot in the Tigers’ potent lineup.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>A one-man billboard for contact rate, Victor Martinez proved in 2013 that he could hit .300 even at 34, even coming off major surgery that cost him a year, even with the footspeed of a woolie mammoth, even coming off the bench as the designated hitter 90% of the time. Though age has robbed him of some power -- he's been below average in isolated slugging percentage for two seasons now -- he's gotten better at making contact as he's aged, too. Which is his most relevant skill. Given that he still has some power, great command of the strike zone, and yes, that tiny little swinging strike rate, there's little reason to think that Martinez can't hit for a great batting average again in 2014. The only problem is that he'll do it as a utility-only player, or a first baseman if you're lucky and your league only requires ten starts for eligibility. Even then, and even with the league's batting average down in the .250s, a one- or two-category first baseman can't be a priority for your team. (<a href="http://www.twitter.com/enosarris">Eno Sarris</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Victor Martinez can still make contact with a little power, and so he can still hit for a good batting average. But now that he's no longer a catcher and no longer has above average power, where will you play him and how much will you pay for that privilege?</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>How much should we buy into V-Mart's power outburst last season? At age 35 ,he hit a career high 32 home runs. He didn't get any more plate appearances compared to the past, either. His fly ball percentage was up, for one. The big difference from previous seasons, though, was a career high 16% home run per fly ball rate. He hit his combined home runs and fly balls seven feet further, but that doesn't seem to be enough to explain the outburst. Tempering expectations will be key for 2015. Regress his home run total back to the 15 to 20 range. If people think he'll hit more than that, let them and move on. His average has always been at or over .300, so expect the same in 2015. In OBP leagues, look for possible drop in his walk rate. He had a career high 28 intentional walks in 2015. If you remove the intentional passes from his last four walks rates, they would be at 6.5%, 6.8%, 6.7%, and 6.9%. Finally, expect no steals and any over one will be a bonus. It's probably best to peg him at 18 homers with a .300 average, no stolen bases, and near 175 runs+RBI. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Don't buy into Victor Martinez's huge jump in home runs -- instead, expect his 2015 total to be between 15 and 20. Also expect some recency bias. He'll likely be overvalued in drafts this season. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Cases like Martinez are why it's tough to buy a post-30 career year. First off, you know the player isn't maintaining that level, especially if they have shown a decade of work well below this new level. Secondly, there is going to be a huge tax after the career year. Too often we justify a buy-in based on the fact that we think they'll at least give us the pre-career year levels. That's where the post-30 factor comes into play. Not only are we staring down the barrel of some serious regression when drafting these guys, but we're also inviting a boatload of injury risk which only adds to the variance of potential results. It came up snake eyes on Martinez in 2015. It won't always play out that way, so this profile doesn't need to be universally ignored, but a dose of skepticism doesn't hurt in these cases. It's why there was so much concern about Nelson Cruz coming into 2015. He went the other way and got even better with a second straight 40 homer season, but the market has only jumped him up a round, round and a half to the 14th outfielder off the board. Martinez is now going nearly 200 picks later than 2015, creating a low-risk buying opportunity. A mild one. (<a href="http://thespguide.com/">Paul Sporer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Martinez slogged through an injury-riddled followup to that career year with his worst season ever. The preseason knee injury hampered him all season, including a month-long disabled list stint in mid-May. Now 37 years old, does he have enough to rebound?</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>V-Mart only walked as often as, and only whiffed 35% less often than, the major league average. That's about the only criticism you can levy against his 2016 season. Yet it may be a legitimate concern: one of modern baseball's best pure hitters may finally be deteriorating. Of course, Martinez's strikeout "problem" is relative; his 2016 plate discipline, arguably the worst of his career, still ranks in the league's upper 10%. In other words, he has a long way to fall. He's hitting the ball as hard as ever, proving his 2014 home run spike wasn't a fluke and legitimizing his late-career power resurgence. Between his still-elite contact skills and apparently above-average power, he could certainly have worse problems as a 38-year-old. Yes, Father Time is coming for Martinez — he won't keep swatting 25 home runs, and his days of hitting .300 are likely behind him — but his high floor could see him descend into his 40s as gracefully as anyone in recent memory or, perhaps, ever, with the standard caveat of "as long as he stays healthy." Twenty home runs and a .280 batting average aren't particularly sexy, but you could do much worse in the middle-to-late rounds, especially if you're intrigued by the typical discount for DH/utility-only hitters. (Alex Chamberlain)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>V-Mart posted arguably the worst plate discipline of his career yet still fared better than 90% of qualified hitters. Even if he's finally declining at 38, his elite contact skills and above-average power have established a comfortable floor for the designated hitter. The possibility of 20 home runs and a .280 average certainly get the job done for those willing to accept his age- and DH-related discounts.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

He's hitting the ball as hard as ever, proving his 2014 home run spike wasn't a fluke and legitimizing his late-career power resurgence. Between his still-elite contact skills and apparently above-average power, he could certainly have worse problems as a 38-year-old. Yes, Father Time is coming for Martinez — he won't keep swatting 25 home runs, and his days of hitting .300 are likely behind him — but his high floor could see him descend into his 40s as gracefully as anyone in recent memory or, perhaps, ever, with the standard caveat of \"as long as he stays healthy.\" Twenty home runs and a .280 batting average aren't particularly sexy, but you could do much worse in the middle-to-late rounds, especially if you're intrigued by the typical discount for DH/utility-only hitters. (Alex Chamberlain)",

Heading into an even year means he should be ready to rock again, right? Hardly.
